House Financial Services Committee Holds Hearing on Monetary Policy Report
LIST OF PANEL MEMBERS AND WITNESSES

PATRICK MCHENRY:
Committee will come to order. Without objection, the chair is authorized to declare a recess of the committee at any time. The hearing is titled the Federal Reserve's Semiannual Monetary Policy Report. We'd like creative titles here, and that is our traditional title for Humphrey Hawkins. Without objection, all members will have five legislative days within which to submit extraneous material to the chair for inclusion in the record.

I'll note at the outset this hearing has a hard stop at 1:00 PM, which we will strictly observe, which seems only humane. I'll now recognize myself for four minutes for an opening statement. Thank you, Chair Powell, for being back with us today. With price -- with prices increasing more than 20 percent since President Biden took office, inflation remains top of mind for American families.

In fact, only a quarter of Americans describe current economic conditions as good or excellent, according to the most recent data from Gallup's Economic Confidence Index. Despite President Biden's gaslighting, inflation was not 9 percent when he took office. It was out of control -- the out of control inflation we're experiencing now is something that this administration did not inherit, but it is a product of their policies and their overspending.

The nearly $2 trillion partisan and fiscally reckless spending as a part of the American Rescue Plan poured fuel on a smoldering inflationary fire. After a delayed response to runaway inflation, the FOMC has acted with a historic pace of interest rate hikes to ease the pain caused by Democrats' failed economic policies.

It is a response, not the actual disease, but the response. The Fed is trying to tame these flames, but it's a response to Democrat policies here on Capitol Hill and the administration. So far this year, inflation has been more persistent than many, including the Fed, previously expected. There's still work to be done to reach the Fed's 2 percent target.

As I've repeatedly said, the Fed's -- I think -- I think as I've repeatedly said, the commitment to independence of the Federal Reserve is of utmost importance, and it is critical especially in a political year like this. Just as -- Chair Powell, just as you did in previous administrations, you must not allow politics to cloud the Fed's monetary policy.

However, despite your best efforts, the Fed independence remains at risk, and unfortunately calls are coming from inside the building. Under Vice Chair Barr, the Fed's regulatory and supervisory agenda has become politicized. Most notably the development of the Basel III endgame proposal has been a mess.

This process has been cloaked in opaque standards and timelines set at meetings of unaccountable global -- global governance bodies. The Fed has promised to process 410 almost universally negative comments, public comments, regarding the unjustified and under-utilized initial proposal -- or an under-analyzed initial proposal.

Recent press reports, which seem to be the only way Congress gets details on the Basel III endgame progress, and your comments yesterday indicate the Fed will finally conduct a long overdue quantitative impact study. That is welcome. And if true, this is a promising, promising development. Chair Powell, the last time you testified before this committee, you stated that the initial Basel III endgame proposal will undergo "broad and material changes." Yesterday in the Senate, you said the board supports reissuing the updated proposal for public comment.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
I'm concerned that press reports also claim the Fed will tuck any changes to the proposal into this quantitative impact study. That study, including the substantial changes to the proposal, would then be issued for public feedback with relatively paltry comment period. I'll reiterate to you here what I've said to you in private. Broad and material changes to the Basel III endgame necessitate a full re-proposal, full stop, full stop.

Failure to do so will result in an immediate Congressional Review Act vote out of this House of Representatives as quickly as we can possibly process it. Now, it doesn't have to be this way, and I think the Fed adhering to its longstanding principles here is highly important, especially with this interagency process.

I'll close with this. Your steady and capable apolitical leadership of the Federal Reserve has shepherded our economy through extreme uncertainty. We now find ourselves in the midst of a new type of uncertainty surrounding the leadership of our nation. Doubts, fears and panic often lead to bad decision making and even worse policy.

I urge you to reject outside political pressure in this volatile time and stay the course for the good of the American people and our economy as a whole. Thank you for your service, and I yield back. I'll now recognize the ranking member of the full committee, Ms. Waters, for four minutes.


MAXINE WATERS:
Thank you very much, Mr. Chairman. Welcome back to the Honorable Jerome H Powell, chairman, Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve System. Chair Powell, as we saw with the latest jobs report, the labor market remains strong and the economy is surging. Despite inheriting an economy from the prior administration that had the worst jobs record since the Great Depression, President Biden has now overseen a record 15.7 million jobs created since he took office, with 206,000 new jobs created just last month.

Not only that but, under the Biden administration, we're witnessing low unemployment rates, rising wages and stabilizing prices for goods and services. This is not only because of President Biden's strong leadership, but also because of the historic legislation signed into law by President Biden which has resulted in lower costs, created more jobs, rebuilt our infrastructure, supported small businesses, eliminated mostly junk fees, wiped out more than $144 billion in federal student loan debt for 14 million borrowers, and cut child poverty in half.

President Biden is and will continue to advance policies that are providing good paying jobs and an economy that works for everyone. Now, while I'm pleased to see that inflation -- inflation is declining, the latest data makes clear that housing remains the number one driver of core inflation. Since 2020, house prices have increased by nearly 50 percent, with Americans now spending on average over 30 percent of their income on housing.

This is a top priority for Democrat, but remains an afterthought for Republicans. Earlier this Congress, I reintroduced my comprehensive legislation housing package, including the Housing Crisis Response Act. This bill provides more than $150 billion in fair and affordable housing investments, representing the single largest investment in affordable housing in our nation's history.

These funds would create nearly 1.4 million affordable and accessible homes, bring down housing costs for all, and revive the American dream of home ownership. Committee Democrats are committed to getting this bill across the finish line and continue to hold out hope that our Republican colleagues will finally join us in this effort.

Unfortunately, extreme MAGA Republicans are not just ignoring housing inflation, they're advancing their Project 2025 manifesto that would dismantle US democracy and the economy as we know it today. Project 2025 is authored by almost two dozen former Trump White House staffers and Trump administration officials.

It was compiled and published by the ultra far right Heritage Foundation, whose CEO recently declared that they are "In the process of a second American Revolution, which will be bloodless if the left allows it to be." And if that kind of talk reminds anyone of the rhetoric we heard in the lead up to and on January 6th, 2020, it should.

Project 2025 promotes radical ideas to materially undermine the Federal Reserve, if not effectively abolish. MAGA wants to put out -- put you out of a job, Chairman Powell. So, I look forward to your testimony and hearing from you, a Republican who was first nominated by President Trump, about your thoughts on the importance of the Federal Reserve and the work you have done to help our economy.

And I yield back.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
The gentlelady yields back. I'd note for the records Jerome Powell was initially nominated by President Obama to the Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve before being nominated by President Trump to chair. So, here we are. We'll recognize the chair of the Financial Institution's Subcommittee, Mr. Barr, the Andy Barr variety, for one minute.


ANDY BARR:
Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Welcome, Chairman Powell. Runaway inflation and increased interest rates necessary to confront it continue to hammer Americans, and especially those living paycheck to paycheck. Workers and families feel the pain making everyday purchases at high prices in the grocery aisles and at the pump.

They have suffered years of eroding purchasing power in their paychecks under the Biden administration's economic mismanagement. Heightened mortgage rates make it prohibitive for new homebuyers to reach the American dream of a starter home for their family. I am pleased that the Fed is resolute in getting inflation under control.

I am pleased that you have committed to an apolitical approach to this. I am not pleased by the Fed's opaque, unjustified, politicized and under-analyzed regulatory proposals, which will ultimately hurt all Americans. We need to know where the Fed is going on its fundamentally flawed Basel III endgame proposal and its opaque regulatory approach.

I yield.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
Well, chair now recognizes the ranking member of the Financial Institutions Monetary Policy Subcommittee, Mr. Foster, for one minute.


BILL FOSTER:
Thank you, Chair McHenry and Ranking Member Waters. I'd like to also thank you, Chair Powell, for joining us today and for the role you have played in combating inflation and supporting a stable economic recovery. While the presidency is often the focus of political pronouncements about macroeconomic conditions, there's no question that that undersells the importance and the independence of the work of the Federal Reserve Board.

While actions taken by the president and Congress certainly play a significant role, monetary policy decisions made by an independent Federal Reserve are the big dog in shaping macroeconomic conditions that shape economic outcomes for millions of Americans. And work continues on inflation, but significant progress has been made to bring the country in line with the Fed's 2 percent target.

The good news is that macroeconomic policy is working as designed. Well-calibrated monetary policy with fiscal support from the American Rescue Plan, the Infrastructure and Jobs Act, and the CHIPS and Science Act has powered strong job creation and low unemployment while staving off a recession that many thought inevitable and far better, I should point out, than our peer countries, while Democrats are committed to supporting your efforts to cut costs for American families.

I yield back.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
We'll now welcome the testimony of Jerome Powell, the 16th chair of the Federal Reserve Board of Governors. Chair Powell, you'll be recognized for five minutes to give an oral presentation of your testimony. Without -- without objection, your written statement will be made a part of the record. You're now recognized for five minutes.


JEROME POWELL:
Chairman McHenry, Ranking Member Waters, and other members of the committee, I appreciate the opportunity to present the Federal Reserve's semiannual monetary policy report. The Federal Reserve remains squarely focused on our dual mandate to promote maximum employment and stable prices for the benefit of the American people.

Over the past two years, the economy has made considerable progress toward the Federal Reserve's 2 percent inflation goal, and labor market conditions have cooled while remaining strong. Reflecting these developments, the risks to achieving our employment and inflation goals are coming into better balance.

I will review -- review the current economic situation before turning to monetary policy. Recent indicators suggest that the US economy continues to expand at a solid pace. Gross domestic product growth appears to have moderated in the first half of this year, following impressive strength in the second half of last year.

Private domestic demand remains robust, however, with slower but still solid increases in consumer spending. We've also been moderate -- we've also seen moderate growth in capital spending and a pickup in residential investment so far this year. Improving supply conditions have supported resilient demand and the strong performance of the US economy over the past year.

In the labor market, a broad set of indicators suggests that conditions have returned to about where they stood on the eve of the pandemic, strong but not overheated. The unemployment rate has moved higher, but was still at a low level of 4.1 percent in June. Payroll job gains averaged 222,000 jobs per month in the first half of the year.

Strong job creation over the past couple of years has been accompanied by an increase in the supply of workers, reflecting increases in labor force participation among individuals aged 25 to 54 and a strong pace of immigration. As a result, the jobs to workers gap is well down from its peak and now stands just a bit above its 2019 level.

Nominal wage growth has eased over the past year. The strong labor market has helped narrow longstanding disparities in employment and earnings across demographic groups. Inflation has eased notably over the past couple of years, but remains above the committee's longer run goal of 2 percent. Total PCE prices rose 2.6 percent over the 12 months ending in May. Core PCE prices, which exclude the volatile food and energy categories, also increased 2.6 percent.

After a lack of progress toward our 2 percent inflation objective in the early part of this year, the most recent monthly readings have shown modest further progress. Longer term inflation expectations appear to remain well anchored, as reflected in a broad range of surveys of households, businesses and forecasters, as well as measures from financial markets.

Our monetary policy actions are guided by our dual mandate to promote maximum employment and stable prices for the American people. In support of these goals, the committee has maintained the target range for the federal funds rate at 5.25 to 5.5 percent since last July, after having tightened the stance of monetary policy significantly over the previous year and a half.

We have also continued to reduce our securities holdings. At our May meeting, we decided to slow the pace of balance sheet runoff starting in June consistent with the plans released previously. Our restrictive monetary policy stance is helping to bring demand and supply into better balance and to put downward pressure on inflation.

The committee has stated that we do not expect it will be appropriate to reduce the target range for the federal funds rate until we have gained greater confidence that inflation is moving sustainably toward 2 percent. Incoming data for the first quarter of this year did not support such greater confidence.

The most recent inflation readings, however, have shown some modest further progress, and more good data would strengthen our confidence that inflation is moving sustainably toward 2 percent. We continue to make decisions meeting by meeting. We know that reducing policy restraint too soon or too much could stall or even reverse the progress that we've seen on inflation.

At the same time, in light of the progress made both in lowering inflation and in cooling the labor market over the past two years, elevated inflation is not the only risk we face. Reducing policy restraint too late or too little could unduly weaken economic activity and employment. In considering adjustments to the target range for the federal funds rate, the committee will continue its practice of carefully assessing incoming data and their implications for the evolving outlook, the balance of risks, and the appropriate path of monetary policy.

Congress has entrusted the Fed with the operational independence that is needed to take a longer term perspective in the pursuit of our dual mandate of maximum employment and price stability. We remain committed to bringing inflation back down to our 2 percent goal and to keeping longer term inflation expectations well anchored.

Restoring price stability is essential to achieving maximum employment and stable prices over the long run. Our success in delivering on those goals matters to all Americans. I'll conclude by emphasizing that we understand that our actions affect communities, families, and businesses across the country. Everything we do is in service to our public mission.

Thank you. I look forward to your questions.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
Well, thank you, Chair Powell. I'll now recognize myself for -- for five minutes. Chair Powell, let -- let's begin with this question of the Basel III endgame. I know you got questions about it yesterday, but I think we need further clarity. Your testimony about Vice Chair Barr's discussions with the FDIC and with the OCC on the next steps for the Basel III endgame, you mentioned potential changes have been made to the original Basel III endgame and that a lot of progress has been made, and that the board is very close to agreeing on the substance of those changes.

But the next steps, that's really what I want to get into. Let's get through the mechanics of what happens next. If the Fed, the OCC and the FDIC agree on the substance of whatever changes you're going to make, um, what happens then? Like walk through the mechanics as you anticipate it?


JEROME POWELL:
OK, um, so we are -- we're very close to having exactly that agreement on the substance of the proposed changes pursuant to those talks. Uh, the next question then is how to proceed. And um, you know, it is my view and the strong view of -- of a number of board members that the appropriate thing to do is to take that new proposal and publish it along with the effects of the quantitative impact survey and put that out for comment again and receive comment on that and then take some time to review those comments before finalizing the regulation.

And, um, that's the discussion we're having with the other two agencies now. We have not been able to reach agreement on a path to do that, but that's something that we're, you know, we think is the right way to do that's. That's what we've done in -- in similar situations, there aren't that many similar situations.

But when we see broader material changes to an important regulation, we think let's go out again and give all the commenters another chance to comment.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
Yeah, and that also avoids serious lawsuit risk and the risk that Congress step in and overturn this rulemaking using the Congressional Review Act. I think the other agencies here, if they're not agreeing with the Fed, they're running against the independence of the Federal Reserve here. This is not a trifling matter of policy.

This is a matter of real substance for the independence of the Fed and rule making at the Federal Reserve, um, which I think we should be able to keep. These, keep the independence of the fed separate um especially in this political environment. But opening it up to a new comment period, um, so that means you have to get consensus for the board of governors on the policy.

You have to get the agreement of the scandal-plagued chair of the FDIC or the -- the five member board of the FDIC. You have to get agreement by the acting comptroller of the currency. These people should not have real standing against with the -- with the Fed on a matter of serious policy, especially with a chair of the FDIC that is being ousted by his own party as soon as they can get a replacement confirmed.

It's an absurd thing that the Fed has to go to an acting comptroller of the currency and a guy who's going to be out of a job very soon at the FDIC and get agreement. Um, but thank you for listening. That was more of a -- to convey this. So the quantitative impact analysis is this going to include the interplay with the stress tests?

Uh, GSIB surcharge, all the other capital charges here, will that be a part of the quantitative impact analysis?


JEROME POWELL:
It will be this proposal, which does include changes to the GSIB surcharge, but does not include the stress test.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
OK, um, but -- but the goal here with the quantitative impact is to actually measure the impact of these rules as enacted, right? Based upon what is existing regulatory structures. OK.


JEROME POWELL:
That's right.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
All right. So let's get into the question of balance sheet as quickly as we can. You two-and-a-half years ago, um, you know, you stated quote, "The committee stand -- the committee intends to slow and then stop the decline in the size of the balance sheet when reserve balances are somewhat above the level it judges to be consistent with ample reserves." Um, that's two-and-a-half years ago, where are we in this question of what -- what are ample reserves?


JEROME POWELL:
Well, so the balance sheet, the run off in the portfolio is now I think $1.7 trillion so far. So we've made quite a lot of progress. We think we have a good ways to go. And as -- as I mentioned and as you just mentioned, we've now slowed the pace really with a view to getting as far as we can without creating frictions and disruption that would -- might cause us to prematurely stop shrinking.

Going a little bit slower might actually enable us to go further. We think we have quite a ways to go. It's very hard to be precise about it. It's really a question of supply and demand and we will find that that level with a little bit of a buffer on top of it and that's where we'll stop.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
All right, thank you for your testimony. Uh, the ranking member, Ms. Waters is recognized for five minutes.


MAXINE WATERS:
Thank you very much. Chairman Powell, are you familiar with Project 2025?


JEROME POWELL:
Not really, no.


MAXINE WATERS:
You've heard about it.


JEROME POWELL:
I really don't focus on these things at all.


MAXINE WATERS:
You don't focus, but you know there's something known as a Project 2025. The reason I'm asking you is because one proposal is to get rid of the Fed's dual mandate to promote only stable prices, but also maximum employment -- employment. This is your mandate. These are mandates, right? This is what you do, is that right?


JEROME POWELL:
We do serve a dual mandate.


MAXINE WATERS:
That's right and so if there was anything that would get rid of the mandate, what would it do to our economy, What would it do to our country?


JEROME POWELL:
So the question of which mandate we serve is very much a question for Congress. Our own -- my own view has been that the dual mandate has served us well. This is something Congress can change and change back to a single mandate. There are --


MAXINE WATERS:
-- What do you do to get maximum employment? What do you do?


JEROME POWELL:
Well, um, we basically we have one tool on the economy and that is we raise and lower interest rates. You'll find though that anyway -- we certainly at the fed do believe that -- that the dual mandate has -- has been a good thing and it's enabled us to -- it has not stopped us from controlling inflation when that was the thing that needed to be done.


MAXINE WATERS:
And so while of course, I understand you may not have seen, heard or read Project 2025, are you familiar with a effort in the country to get rid of diversity and inclusion?


JEROME POWELL:
I see these things mentioned, but honestly we're, you know, we're pretty focused on our task which is -- which is maximum employment and price stability. We're strong supporters of -- of diversity at the Fed as you know.


MAXINE WATERS:
Are you aware that I created a subcommittee on diversity and employment in this committee?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes, I remember that.


MAXINE WATERS:
And do you think that, um, getting rid of diversity and inclusion interferes with your ability to really realize the mandate of maximum employment?


JEROME POWELL:
I -- so I've -- you know, I was uh spent most of my career in the private sector and what I observed was really successful institutions in the United States, companies, organizations generally are those that do a really good job on diversity and get the best out of people. And attract a broad, diverse range of talents to the table.

And people feel comfortable speaking. So that's -- that's the way we feel about it at the Fed and that's -- that's what we've been doing and we'll continue to do.


MAXINE WATERS:
And do you think it's important not only to have diversity and inclusion, um, in the public sector, but in the private sector also?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes, and as I mentioned, if you look at very successful American companies, you will very often see that they're -- they're good at that they're good at hiring, attracting, investing in and keeping diverse talent. That's one of the things that are really good US public companies do well.


MAXINE WATERS:
Have you seen improvement during your tenure where diversity and inclusion has created opportunities for more jobs and have to reduce the unemployment rate?


JEROME POWELL:
Certainly seen, um, uh, I think you see over the course of my long career, a big change in diversity and inclusion, um, and you see that in the private sector and the public sector. And I think that's generally progress. I do.


MAXINE WATERS:
Uh, under the work of this committee and the subcommittee is chaired by Ms. Beatty, she was able to gather important data about what was going on in the private sector. And what she discovered was that many of the CEOs and others welcomed the opportunity to learn more and to do better and to get assistance.

And we start -- we saw improvement with diversity and inclusion. Have you seen that?


JEROME POWELL:
There's no question, if you talk to CEOs, they -- they get this. If you want to attract the best talent in -- in our country now you need to be -- you need to be committed to these things.


MAXINE WATERS:
And so I would like to compliment you on the job that you have been doing. I'd like to compliment you on keeping us informed about inflation. Not only do I welcome you here today, I look forward to working with you for years to come. I yield back. Thank you.


JEROME POWELL:
Thank you.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
The gentleman from Arkansas, the vice chair of the full committee, Mr. Hill is now recognized for five minutes.


FRENCH HILL:
I thank the chair. Chair Powell. Thanks so much and welcome back to the committee. I want to pick up where Senator Tim Scott and Chair McHenry left off and reiterate my strong support for your comment yesterday in the Senate about the need to re-propose the Basel III endgame. The Supreme Court's precedent I think makes clear that if a rule undergoes broad and material changes from the proposal to final rule, the public must be giving a meaningful opportunity to review and comment on those changes.

You generally share that view, I think is that right?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes.


FRENCH HILL:
Um, and I'm looking forward to seeing the results of the quantitative impact study and the separate comment period as well as an interagency agreement you referenced yesterday because of Dodd-Frank's role in the vice chair for supervision, Vice Chairman Barr's role and the Fed's role. Is it -- would it be fair to say the Fed is a first among equals on proposing a rule like this?

In other words, does the Fed have a supremacy position on determining whether it should be fully re-proposed or not or do you view it strictly as a collaborative basis? Just curious in your view on that.


JEROME POWELL:
I would say it's strictly collaborative and I would say that our discussions with the FDIC, which -- which Vice Chair Barr has actually been conducting and the OCC, they've been very productive so far. So I want to make sure to say that we've -- we've continued to work our way through this and I -- I believe we will get fairly soon to -- to a resolution of the remaining process issue.


FRENCH HILL:
Good. Well, let me -- let me turn from that subject to the court's recent decision to overturn the so-called Chevron doctrine. Many of us believe this was the first step to reining in decades literally of an unprecedented, uncontrolled growth in the administrative state. And so I think all of us on at least this side of the aisle are certainly saying to the Federal Reserve and other federal agencies in our jurisdiction that we want to reassert Article I authority over the direction that independent agencies work.

Would it be fair to ask you to certify that because of this change in Chevron that the Fed would commit to promulgating new rules only if they're at the direction of an explicit Congressional authorization?


JEROME POWELL:
So I -- I think, um, first of all we're -- we're studying that and several other decisions that have just come down in the last week or two. So I haven't got anything definitive for you on that. I think you know us to be an organization, I know us to be an organization that is strongly committed to the rule of law.

Supreme Court says what the law is, we'll always do what we believe the law is.


FRENCH HILL:
Well, I'll submit that question maybe in more detail in writing and maybe you'll have a chance to reflect on that. Back in February you were on 60 Minutes and you said the US budget deficit, the national debt are unsustainable. Do you still view that the US is on an unsustainable fiscal path?


JEROME POWELL:
I do. So I was -- I think I tried to be clear that the level of the US debt is not itself unsustainable, but the path that we're on is unsustainable and I think that is not controversial.


FRENCH HILL:
I think many of us certainly agree with that and we know that when the -- the deficits at three times the economic growth rate and growing, it's -- it's a concern and it's contributed to inflation. Just three years ago in Jackson Hole, Wyoming, you gave a speech where you were confident that inflation was transitory, which I think we've come to realize that's not the case.

This hearing sort of a can't miss opportunity for the fed to demonstrate some humility on the monetary policy decisions which some on this side of the aisle particularly think have made inflation worse. Your August 2020 Jackson Hole speech, you said the flexible average inflation targeting framework regarding 2 percent, that would -- you would let it run above 2 percent.

Was the Fed blinded by the previous 20 years of -- of global change that was deflationary and not -- you were not alert enough in 2020 to be more cautious about that change in policy?


JEROME POWELL:
So we were certainly mindful of a long period of time in which there have been very low-interest rates, but also very low inflation suggesting that the neutral interest rate must have fallen quite substantially. That was the standard view. Um, the thing we didn't see coming was the pandemic. It's not like everything went off the rails.

It's like we had this pandemic and it really changed the way the economy is working. We had a big crisis. We did a lot of things. So the concerns that led us to -- those concerns that we were in a world of very low interest rates all the time.


FRENCH HILL:
But now wouldn't you say we're in a very opposite situation where because of reshoring and tariffs and other policies that are quite inflationary?


JEROME POWELL:
I was -- well, I would say this, those concerns are right now we have the policy rate in the -- in the mid fives, right? And -- and we don't see -- we see that policy is restrictive. But clearly interest rates, the neutral interest rate must have moved up at least in the short term. So I think that's the question we'll be asking ourselves in our review which begins at the end of this year --


FRENCH HILL:
-- We look -- we look forward to that. I yield back to the chairman --


JEROME POWELL:
-- is how much of what we did in -- in the how much of what we did in -- in -- in that time period is irrelevant to the new world where rates appear to be higher.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
The gentleman from Georgia, Mr. Scott is recognized for five minutes.




DAVID SCOTT:
Thank you, Chairman and welcome, Chair Powell. It's great seeing you. Last time I saw you, you had -- we had the gracious pleasure of you visiting with me in my office and we discussed a great variety of things. Thank you for that visit. Now this year, the Fed tested 31 banks up from 23 last year, is that correct?


JEROME POWELL:
I believe that's right, yes.


DAVID SCOTT:
And by estimating losses, revenues, expenses and capital level under hypothetical stress scenarios, we did that correct?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes.


DAVID SCOTT:
And all 31 banks remained above their minimum common equity tier one capital requirements after observing losses of nearly $685 billion. Is that correct?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes, that is correct.


DAVID SCOTT:
Yeah, I wanted to get those figures out and provide you with, uh, you may have heard this, but I want to share it with the nation because of your Vice Chair Barr made this statement and I wanted to put this in the record his exact quote from this great achievement, he said, "Our goal of our stress test is to help ensure that we have enough capital to absorb losses in a highly stressful scenario and this test shows that we do." I thought that was a great statement, uh, of your record.

Now let me ask you my question, it's simple will the 2024 Fed stress test results have an impact on how prudential regulators roll out a new and updated capital proposal?


JEROME POWELL:
So the two are really two different things. Um, there's the -- there's the Basel III capital proposal and then there's the stress test. So -- and really the Basel III, uh, as I mentioned, we've discussed, we're almost ready to put forward for further comment, a revised proposal with material and broad changes to it. The stress tests are a different thing.

And of course we tried it, we realize we have to adapt those over time and be open to changes. And we -- we, you know, we have -- it has evolved significantly over time, but it's really a separate thing from the Basel III endgame.


DAVID SCOTT:
Give us a little bit more information on Basel III because I worked with you with this. Our work goes all the way back to the Obama administration when we responded to that crisis with, uh, our banks. And you and I worked that up where we came up with the Hardest Hit program to help those who were suffering with -- those states that were suffering with high unemployment and at the same time high home foreclosures and we were successful and we've established that program and it's still going on and helping many of our states.

But, uh, you know, I want to also share what is happening around the world as a result of our activities. The European Union is now set to delay key parts of its bank capital rules by more than a year so that their leaders -- lenders will not be at a disadvantage. Over in Canada, it's important to know that their banking regulators have also delayed for another year imposing high capital rules on country's banks at the risk of making them uncompetitive.

And the Swiss National Bank is highly unlikely now to adopt a proposed 15 percent capital requirement for UPS and other Swiss banks. We -- what we do in the end will be consistent with the -- with the Basel agreement and it will also be consistent with what other comparable large jurisdictions are doing.

So, can you elaborate how the expansion in the money supply and fiscal stimulus played a role in persistent inflation?

And why is it so important to get to the 2 percent inflation rate goal right now?


JEROME POWELL:
So, the -- the inflation that arose here was a collision between very strong demand as the economy reopened. Remember that there had been fiscal transfers. We had rates very low. You know, those things were done because we -- we thought we could be looking at a very, very bad economic time. As it turns out, the economy reopened and demand spiked very high.

People had saved a lot of money because they couldn't spend. And so, there was tremendous demand and there was constrained supply. And what you got was inflation, and you got that everywhere in the world. That's what happened. So, what's happened to bring inflation down, we've made quite a lot of progress on inflation.

To bring inflation down, the Fed has been working on -- with restrictive policy, on cooling demand. That has been working. And the supply side has been healing. And, you know, the -- the negative labor participation shock has essentially reversed adjusted for inflation.

JEROME POWELL:

This includes helping to make sure that financial services are affordable and accessible to everyone. As the Fed moves forward with the proposed changes to Regulation II, I know that protecting access to affordable and accessible banking is at the top of your mind. That is why I was concerned when I started receiving outreach about the unintentional effects that Reg II proposed rule would have on marginalized communities, including my constituents in Atlanta.

Specifically, I heard about the impact that the reduction in interchange fees proposed by this rule could have on bank owned certified accounts in my district and across the country. The bank owned initiative is a partnership between financial institutions and trusted community based organizations to offer low or no cost bank accounts to unbanked and underbanked individuals.

Access and affordability are at the heart of the changes that you're proposing, and I couldn't agree more with those goals. But we have to make sure that programs designed to serve unbanked and underbanked individuals in marginalized communities continue to flourish. In response to these concerns, my colleague across the aisle, Mr. Luetkemeyer and I sent a bipartisan comment letter in March to you highlighting those very concerns and urging that the final rule not negatively impact low and moderate income communities.

Chair Powell, how does the Fed take into account how regulations impact constituents who don't have an attorney or an interest group to submit a comment on their behalf?


JEROME POWELL:
Well, let me say first we -- you know, we have heard those concerns that you raise and others have raised about aspects of the proposed rule. And, you know, we very much understand the concerns that are being raised. In terms of people who -- where we don't have comment letters, you know, we -- we -- I mean, we try to be thoughtful about the impact of our regulations.

But principally, we're looking for public comment on -- on things.


NIKEMA WILLIAMS:
Thank you. The FDIC'S most recent data shows that the rate of unbanked households is at an all time low. The bank owned initiative has certainly played an important role in this progress. Bank owned certified accounts are available to more than 95 percent of low and moderate income households across all 50 states.

Access to low or no cost banking services is a -- is a door to financial inclusion and wealth generation for marginalized families. As written, Reg II could undo some of those -- some of the enormous progress made in the past several years. Chair Powell, how can regulators work with members of this committee to ensure that future proposed rules do not hinder Americans' access to tools that enhance the Federal Reserve's financial inclusion efforts?


JEROME POWELL:
So, we're -- we're very focused on things like bank owned, as you pointed out, and on -- on, you know, access to the financial system for marginalized communities. And we wouldn't want to do anything to -- to weigh on that. And -- and we -- we're happy to work with you and your office on -- on -- on that -- on those issues.

But we're -- so, you know, as I mentioned, we're -- we're hearing the comments and we're reading them. And we're -- you know, we're taking those into account as we think about appropriate changes to the proposal.


NIKEMA WILLIAMS:
So, we will definitely follow up on that because, as we're writing these regulations, I want to make sure that -- that we're thinking about the real world impact and what it means to consumers when they're accessing financial institutions. I look forward to working with you to further the Fed's financial inclusion efforts.

Like, earlier this week, I sent you another bipartisan letter related to the Federal Reserve's April propose -- proposal to extend the operating hours of Fedwire Funds Service to 22 hours per day, seven days per week and every day of the year. That was news that I was eager to hear. This may sound like a small technical change, but it can have a big impact on our constituents, especially for those living in marginalized communities who live paycheck to paycheck.

Growing up in the booming metropolis of Smiths Station, Alabama, probably never heard of it, Chair Powell, but there are limited options for loans and short term loans. In an area where everyone needs a car to get to work and they have to work to eat, when money is short, any delay in accessing a paycheck or paying a bill can disrupt your entire household.

Extending operating hours for Fedwire Funds Service and National Settlement Service will allow people continual access to their funds outside of what we deem normal operating hours. And I hear the gavel. I'm out of time, but we will be following up for more conversation. Thank you.


JEROME POWELL:
Thank you.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
The gentleman from Nebraska, Mr. Flood, is recognized for five minutes.


MIKE FLOOD:
Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Chairman Powell, after a year with very high inflation that battered American paychecks, inflation rates have moderated some. However, inflation continues to linger above the 2 percent target rate set by the Federal Reserve. In fact, the PCE inflation index has remained above 2 percent for the last three years.

The Federal Open Market Committee Statement on Longer Run Goals and Monetary Policy Strategy issued at the beginning of the year stated, "The committee seeks to achieve inflation that averages 2 percent over time and therefore judges that, following periods when inflation has been running persistently below 2 percent, appropriate monetary policy will likely aim to achieve inflation moderately above 2 percent for some time." Chairman Powell, does the commitment to achieve a 2 percent average over time apply in the other direction as well?

Meaning that, if inflation remains above 2 percent for a protracted period, would the committee instead aim for inflation moderately below 2 percent for some time in order to achieve an inflation average of 2 percent?


JEROME POWELL:
No, it doesn't.


MIKE FLOOD:
Is it fair to say that you would need to see the PCE index dip below 2 percent at least once in the coming months in order to contemplate a rate cut?


JEROME POWELL:
No, that's not fair to say. We -- we've said that you -- you don't want to wait until inflation gets all the way down to 2 percent, because inflation has a certain momentum. You wouldn't -- you wouldn't wait that long. If you -- if you waited that long, you've probably waited too long because inflation will be moving downward and will go well below 2 percent, which we don't want.


MIKE FLOOD:
What combination of factors would you need to see in order to support a rate cut this month or in September?


JEROME POWELL:
So, I'm -- once again, I'm -- I'll say I'm not sending any signals on any particular date of any meeting whatsoever on policy. I said that yesterday and I'm saying it again today. But I will answer your question. So, what we've said is that we want to be more confident. We want to have greater confidence, and that means more good inflation readings that inflation is moving sustainably down to 2 percent, greater confidence that that is the case.

But remember, we have a dual mandate, too. We're not just an inflation targeting central bank. We -- we also have an employment mandate. So, I -- I could also see us cutting, and we've said this, I've said this, if we saw unexpected weakening in the labor market. And we do now see -- I -- I'll speak for myself.

I now see the -- the risks to the two mandates as much closer to being in balance. I think for a long time we've had to focus heavily on the inflation mandate. But I think now we're getting to the place where -- where, you know, your -- your -- the -- the labor market is -- is getting pretty much in balance to where it needs to be. And -- and so, we're looking at both sides.


MIKE FLOOD:
I know that the media and others have continued to talk about the prospect of political interference with the Federal Reserve. With an election coming up, I know you are very aware of the heightened scrutiny awaiting the Open Market Committee meetings in July and September. Can you use this opportunity to speak regarding the Federal Reserve's political independence going into this election?


JEROME POWELL:
Sure, I'd be glad to. So, you know, our political independence is -- is critical to our ability to -- to do our jobs and -- and to sustain the faith of people across the political spectrum. And it comes down to we make our decisions based on economic data, the evolving outlook, the balance of risks, and we don't take into consideration other -- any other factors, including political factors.

We have a long history of -- of -- of doing that, and I think the public believes we will do that. Any decision that we make to -- you know, on rates, on -- on any of our policy tools, it's going to be very well-grounded in the data, and it will represent our best thinking about what is best for the American public in the near and medium term.

And that's the -- that's the promise that I will give and that all my colleagues will give. And that means we're -- we're not looking at things like election cycles. We're not looking at -- at any of those things. We're looking at the data, what does it tell us is the right thing to do. When we figure that out, when we think it's time to move we go ahead and move, but not until then.


MIKE FLOOD:
Appreciate that. Finally, I'd like to raise concerns with housing costs. While shelter is one component of the broader PCE calculation, housing often makes up one of the largest, if not the largest, expense for many consumers. Inflation for housing remains persistently high. One of the characteristics of this economy is that consumer -- consumer sentiment is remarkably low.

Do you think high housing costs could be contributing to the persistently low consumer sentiment?


JEROME POWELL:
I think prices -- high prices generally. I think nobody really has a -- can be super highly confident of their -- of their answer on this. But I do think it's the fact that, while inflation has come down, prices are high, if you know what I mean, and -- and people are paying more for things, more for housing, more for the essentials of life, food, energy.

And, you know, that -- that to me is -- is how I would explain surveys. We say that the economy is growing, inflation's come down, unemployment's low. And all that's true, but prices are high.


MIKE FLOOD:
Thank you, Chairman Powell, for your answer and your testimony. And I yield back.


PATRICK MCHENRY:
The gentleman from New York, Mr. Torres, is recognized for five minutes.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Thank you. Chair Powell, the Fed has an inflation rate target of 2 percent. Are you waiting for both PCE inflation and CPI inflation to fall to 2 percent, or only one of those metrics?


JEROME POWELL:
So, we -- we look at different measures. But for a quarter of a century, the PCE inflation has been the Fed's goal. We've defined our goal in terms of that because we think it's -- it's the better measure of the -- of the costs and inflation that the public actually faces.


RITCHIE TORRES:
And now, you've said that you're willing to cut interest rates before reaching the 2 percent target. Is the decision to cut interest rates going to be driven by reaching a particular target en route to 2 percent, or is it driven by the overall trajectory of the inflation rate?


JEROME POWELL:
It's -- it's going to be driven by the totality of the data. There isn't a -- there isn't a particular number that we have in mind that we have to get to. It's more -- we look at all of the data, and the question we're asking ourselves is are we sufficiently confident that inflation really is moving down toward 2 percent?

So, what's the underlying inflation rate looking through the volatility. We're also looking, though, as I mentioned, at -- at the labor market. And we're asking ourselves, you know, we -- we have to take into account now maximum employment, that mandate. So, we're looking at both of those in the decisions that we make.


RITCHIE TORRES:
I mean, are you confident that the inflation rate is on a downward trajectory?


JEROME POWELL:
I -- I do have some confidence of that. I think we've seen that over the past several years. The question is are we sufficiently confident that it is moving sustainably down to 2 percent, and I'm not prepared to say that yet.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Chair Powell, you announced a re-proposal of Basel III rather than a mere revision. Do you believe that the US banking system is sufficiently capitalized in the absence of Basel III?


JEROME POWELL:
I -- I've long been of the view that US -- that US banks are well capitalized and that the level of capital in the US banking system is -- is about right.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Okay. So, if the banking system is sufficiently capitalized in the absence of Basel III, then what exactly is the need for Basel III?


JEROME POWELL:
I think, first of all, there's -- there's no precise answer as to the appropriate level of capital. I think we -- we've been part of developing the Basel standards. They create international broad parity. It's -- it's important that we have that. It's important that we -- that we do something that is comparable to what the other large jurisdictions are doing and that it's consistent with Basel.

And I think that's -- that's what our banks want, that's what we want, and that's -- that will be best serving the public.


RITCHIE TORRES:
But you agree we should conform without gold plating.


JEROME POWELL:
You know, we -- some -- some things we've gold plated and some things we haven't. And -- but I think -- I think we should make -- I think our endgame proposal should be -- at the end should be consistent with the requirements of Basel and -- and consistent with what the other large jurisdictions are doing.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Are you confident that there's no legal conflict between the standardization recommended by Basel III and the regulatory tailoring mandated by Congress?


JEROME POWELL:
I think we can work through all that.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Okay, but if there were a conflict with --


JEROME POWELL:
-- Basel -- Basel doesn't impose any requirements on anyone, there's no enforceable requirements. Every jurisdiction does. What it's going to do. Basel is -- is doesn't bind anybody.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Right, but the regulatory framework you're adopting would codify those recommendations, yes, which would have the force of law, right?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes, that's right.


RITCHIE TORRES:
And so if there were a conflict between the codified recommendations and an act of Congress, would you agree that an act of Congress would supersede those recommendations?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes. Sure.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Does the loss of Chevron deference have any implications for Basel III?


JEROME POWELL:
You know, the -- it's very early days to, to -- to assess there are several decisions that that are about administrative law and I think it's too early for us to say. I mean, ultimately the question is, are the actions we're taking in compliance with the law and that that decision says that there will be a less deference or no deference maybe to the opinions of the agency.

But that just means a court will be making -- they're answering the same question, which is, are those actions consistent with -- with the law.


RITCHIE TORRES:
The Fed has a target rate when it comes to inflation. Does the fed have a target when it comes to quantitative tightening like when it comes to what should be the size of the Fed's balance sheet?


JEROME POWELL:
Well, we have a -- we don't have a specific target, no.


RITCHIE TORRES:
What would you consider to be a healthy size for the Fed?


JEROME POWELL:
We define it, um, you know, with not with numbers but with words and we, you know, we want an ample reserves regime with a buffer so that -- so that reserves are -- are not scarce and we think plentiful reserves, ample reserves is the right place to be. And you know, we'll find that empirically.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Can you put a number on that?


JEROME POWELL:
No, I can't really.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Uh, for -- for decades, we've had the best of both worlds, low unemployment and low inflation. Can the US economy return to the golden age of low unemployment and low inflation or are we doomed to live with a new normal of higher interest rates?


JEROME POWELL:
Well, I -- you know, I think we have low inflation, we've got a period here of very low -- I'm sorry of low -- low -- low unemployment.


RITCHIE TORRES:
We have high inflation. Co can we have the best of both worlds?


JEROME POWELL:
We certainly can and that's the plan. We will -- we're going to return to 2 percent inflation. I'm reasonably confident. We -- some people argue that we're entering into a world of a lot of upward inflation shocks that would be a challenging world. But, you know, that remains to be seen.


RITCHIE TORRES:
Thank you.

BRITTANY PETTERSEN:

People are unable to move because they -- they would go to a much higher interest rate, and then people are unable to buy because they can't afford those mortgage payments. And so, while you've talked about recognizing that lowering the interest rates will actually help with addressing the housing crisis, it's -- we still have a lack of housing supply.

So, can you talk a little bit more about what's happening and that -- you know, that lower interest rates are only a piece of this?


JEROME POWELL:
It's a -- it's a longer term thing. And a lot of it is it's harder to get lots and zoning and materials and workers. And in many, many metropolitan areas, the -- the -- the near-in areas are all built up. If you look around Washington -- I grew up near Washington DC, and, you know, it was countryside just a couple of miles outside the Beltway.

There was no Beltway when I was born. But ultimately, it's just -- we -- we have not enough housing. And that was true before the pandemic, and certainly the pandemic did slow down housing construction. I think we'll get back to a more normal economy with lower interest rates and -- and those sorts of things.

But we're still going to have a, you know, housing shortage.


BRITTANY PETTERSEN:
Absolutely. When I think about the other pieces of the fallout from the global pandemic and the changes that we saw in our economy was in commercial real estate. And I've continued to read about regional bank failures and the risks that the commercial real estate poses in the long term for those assets being on the books for banks.

What do you -- what is your insight on the risk that it poses for financial stability and what we should be thinking about here in Congress?


JEROME POWELL:
So, the -- the commercial real estate situation, which is -- you know, it's -- it's significantly downtown office and related retail and things like that. This is -- this is something that banks have been working their way through for the last couple of -- couple of years. I think it'll take more time, more years to work all the way through it. We know from the stress tests and from our own work that -- that the large bank, they're -- they're going to be okay here.

Some of the regional banks and smaller banks have what you would expect, which is high concentrations in their local community in real estate. We are aware of those. The banks are aware of it. I think the supervisors are -- are -- have been around those banks making sure they have capital, they have liquidity, they have a reasonably -- you know, not too optimistic assessment of how much capital they'll need, how big the losses will be. So, I think we'll be working through this.

And I -- you know, it's -- it doesn't seem to be a systemic problem or one that threatens the broader financial stability. It does threaten bank profitability and -- and those sorts of things. But -- and it'll be with us a while. We'll just keep working our way through it.

And the ability to continue to loan to small businesses --


JEROME POWELL:

SCOTT FITZGERALD:
Right.


JEROME POWELL:

And one of them just is the discount window worked, but we could certainly modernize it and make it more effective.

And also, we learned, you know, that -- that bank runs are moving just a whole lot faster, at least in that case, and that the speed even -- even bank runs from 10 or 15 years ago were -- were nothing like as fast as what happened in Silicon Valley. So, we need to -- we need to bake that -- that new learning into the liquidity requirements in some way or other.

We, again, haven't made a proposal. When we do, of course, it'll go out for comment. And we'll -- we'll -- we'll very much want to learn from -- from -- from those comments.

SCOTT FITZGERALD:

So, given those concerns raised about the lack of transparency in its stress testing models and the potential unintended consequences, is the Fed planning to indirectly regulate non-bank financial institutions through these tests?


JEROME POWELL:
That's -- that's not the idea. The idea is we -- you know, we see intermediation growing very quickly in non-banks. And, you know, we don't regulate them. We're not -- we're don't have a secret plan to regulate them or anything like that. It is -- but the question is what -- what risks are being kept inside the banking system, which we do regulate and supervise, and what are the relationships between these large non-banks and banks.

And, you know, we don't -- we don't have a preconceived answer to that. We just want to understand what are the -- you know, what are those business relationships like and what kind of risks does that mean the banks are running. That's all it is.

The gentlewoman from New York, Ms. Velazquez, is now recognized.


NYDIA VELAZQUEZ:
Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Thank you, Chairman Powell, for being here this morning -- today. It's almost noon. Once the Fed is reassured that inflation is under control, what does the path back to neutral interest rates look like?


JEROME POWELL:
We've said that we wouldn't reduce rates until we were confident that inflation is moving sustainably down to 2 percent. I think the question of what is neutral is -- is -- is going to be an empirical question. I think -- it seems to me it's unlikely that we'll be going back to the very low interest rates of the pre-crisis period.

But we'll know that from -- you know, we won't know that until we -- until we get there, in a way.


NYDIA VELAZQUEZ:
And the Fed's dual mandate comprises of price stability and maximum sustainable employment. As the Fed is thinking about inflation and the possibility of interest rate cuts, how are you balancing these priorities with the need to maximize employment?


JEROME POWELL:
So over the past couple of years, we've had a very strong labor market and inflation well above target and that has led us to focus mainly on bringing down inflation. Over the course of two years, inflation has come down pretty significantly. There's more work to do there. We're not at our target. We need to keep on that job.

At the same time, the labor market has cooled pretty significantly. And so I would say those two goals are now much closer to being in balance. And that means from a policy standpoint, we need to be paying attention to both of them. Whereas for the last couple of years, really, we had to pay mostly attention to inflation.


NYDIA VELAZQUEZ:
Chairman Powell, I have told you that I will be asking for a status update on the section 956 rulemaking at every future hearing. Since your last appearance in March, several of your fellow regulators have issued a proposed rule. Why did the fed choose not to sign onto this proposal?


JEROME POWELL:
So what we're doing is we're looking at the current state of affairs as it relates to incentive compensation. As you may know, we've had guidance in place now since 2010 for all banks, and we supervise pretty significantly around that. So it's a very different picture than the one that was there in 2010. So we're asking ourselves what is the situation today and how do we tailor a proposal to address the residual risk, as opposed to what the situation in 2010 was.


NYDIA VELAZQUEZ:
Yes. In previous hearings, you said that you need to better understand the problem in order to write the rule. In a speech in June, SEC Commissioner Lizarraga said, and I quote, it has been well documented that in the lead up to the financial crisis, pay structures often Encourage big bets that maximize short-term profits, but ignore bigger longer term risks that threaten to take down the entire financial system.

Additionally, since your last appearance, several of your fellow regulators have moved toward with a proposed rule. Why have your fellow regulators and Commissioner Lizarraga been able to understand the scope of the problem and the fed has not?


JEROME POWELL:
Well, I think the quote absolutely makes my point, which is I think everyone agrees that incentive compensation practices before the global financial crisis were not in a good place. However, we published guidance on incentive compensation binding on all, or not binding, but guidance for all banks in 2010, after putting it out for comment.

A lot of thought went into that guidance and now we supervise in that guidance. And so the situation with incentive compensation now in banks is completely different than it was before the global financial crisis, completely different.


NYDIA VELAZQUEZ:
Well, the other regulators have been able to figure this out and you haven't. But let me just say, this is not how congressional mandates work. Of all the rule making provisions in the Dodd-Frank, only 148 were mandatory and of those, only 22 had a deadline of less than a year after enactment. Section 956 was one of them, and I hope that you come to the conclusion that it is your duty to issue the regulation.

Thank you and I yield back, Mr. Chairman.


JEROME POWELL:
Section 156 requires either a rule or guidance, by the way. It does not require a rule, a rule or a guidance. Thank you.

JEROME POWELL:

But I think my sense and pretty commonly, people think we probably won't go back to that era, between the global financial crisis and the pandemic where rates were very, very low and inflation was very low.

And extremely low. There were major European countries that had negative 10-year bond rates, negative, and that was not the case here. But I don't think we're going back to rates that are that low. We think that things like the neutral rate are driven by slow moving forces. But ultimately, you can see the effect.

Our policy rate is over five percent now and it feels like policy is restrictive but not intensely restrictive, so that that suggests that the neutral rate of interest at least as of now will have risen somewhat, which means rates will be a little higher.

JEROME POWELL:

It's a source for banks that they can use under certain circumstances. But we know that that the infrastructure is a little tired and we're investing in that and making it more user friendly and all that.

So that's a big, big project that's going on. Sorry, the second point was stigma. So stigma, that's tough. That's a tough one. And it amounts to, there are a lot of ways to get after that. We're studying all of them. In a sense, if you require banks to use the discount window, then that can help with the stigma.

I mean, this is a big ask, but when congress required us to publish the names of discount window users, that didn't help. It doesn't help at all because banks basically say we're not going to use discount window because people might see us as troubled and that's not what we want. We want people to be able to freely use the discount window.

So we've been focused on this issue for a long time, have not made a lot of progress on it, but right now, I think we're very focused on it.

AL GREEN:

I do want to ask you about several things, and I hope that I'll get to them. But first, there was a post failure lessons report after the failure of Silicon Valley and Signature banks. I thought that report was pretty important. How important is that post failure lessons report?


JEROME POWELL:

AL GREEN:

I'd like to see them exempt from that.

Do you have any comments on it?


JEROME POWELL:
That's either a statutory matter or it's a matter of the FDIC's practice. So it's not something the fed has any input into, really.


AL GREEN:
OK. Let's do this next, please. As time runs out, uh, would you kindly finish your commentary? You were giving a comment on the pandemic and how the pandemic helped to generate this inflation and you were stopped in the middle of your comment. So would you go back through that, please, so that the public at large can get a better understanding of what actually happened with the pandemic and inflation?


JEROME POWELL:
So I'd be glad to, and I think people are now--we have a few years to look back. I think the more years that pass, the clearer we can see what was happening. But I think you look back now and you're seeing this--broadly, what was happening was governments did a lot to support economic activity during the pandemic.

And on the theory that there could be a really serious economic bad times ahead. Then the economy reopened, and it reopened very, very strongly. And I think in hindsight, you can see that there was just a lot of support for demand from fiscal policy, from monetary policy, and that supply was constrained.

You couldn't make cars. Supply chains were tangled up. There were shortages of so many things. So what happened was we got a burst of inflation, and the United States got a big burst of inflation, really more from demand than other countries. Did then you have the war in Ukraine, which gave a big burst of inflation, more to Europe than to us and so you wind up with a situation where you've just got a lot of inflation.

And our thinking, my thinking at the time was it's going to take restrictive monetary policy and it's going to take time for the supply side and demand side distortions from the pandemic to unwind. 2023 was the year when that kind of happened. So supply chains were fixed, the labor shortage was greatly alleviated, and unemployment remained very low.

Inflation came down by a very large amount last year, while growth remained quite strong. So this was the year that kind of proved that thesis. Now we're in 2024 and it's a question of finishing the job on inflation, which we're committed to do, while also keeping a strong labor market, which we're also committed to doing.

And that's a balance that we have to strike in our policy. But I think we know more now about where this came from because we can see what made it go away. And it was a combination of supply and demand, as we had kind of expected.


AL GREEN:
And two of the most significant factors were the pandemic and the war with Ukraine, is that correct?


JEROME POWELL:
Yes, and it's also the fact that the pandemic summoned forth a great fiscal and monetary response and that contributed to really strong activity and to inflation, without question.


AL GREEN:
We're going to run out of time, but I have to ask, but if not for that monetary response, I know that it's a counterfactual, but would we have possibly gone into a depression?


JEROME POWELL:
That's what we thought at the time, and I think mainstream economists were very concerned that--we'd never literally shut the global economy down for a period and then tried to reopen it. We didn't know how long it would take or how well that would go.


AL GREEN:
Thank you, Mr. Chairman.

As you and I have discussed before, I started out as young farmer in 1977 in that inflationary period during the Carter years, then when we went through Chairman Volcker's rather, shall we say, dramatic tightening of monetary policy, and I still remember paying 17 percent to borrow calf feed money when I was a student in college.

I was well collateralized, but that was a bargain in the fact that the capital's accessible. JEROME POWELL:
So, I -- I think, really, one of the big lessons coming out of the big -- the high inflation of the '70s, which we both lived through, is that it really is on the central bank to be on the case and do the job and -- and make sure that it is fully well and truly done, and -- and that -- really, that is up to the central bank.

And believe it or not, that wasn't fully accepted or that wasn't necessarily the thinking. And also, the independence of central banks was much less respected back then. So, all the more credit to Paul -- Paul Volcker for having the -- the courage to do it. So, we -- we now -- that is an internalized lesson for people in central banking these days.

We do understand that. We are committed to bringing inflation sustainably down to 2 percent.

JEROME POWELL:

We want to get inflation down to 2 percent, but we want to keep a strong labor market too.

BILL FOSTER:

You know, I guess when things are going well it's -- as they currently are, it's all of our duties to look around the curve and see it -- the risks that keep you up at night. So, I appreciate Representative Luetkemeyer's question. Now, over the last few years, there have been increasing interest in synthetic risk transfers, SRTs, or credit linked notes, which are often used by US banks to shift risk away from the banking system and as a means of managing regulatory capital.



BILL FOSTER:
So, I've been concerned by recent reports that the buyers of some of these SRTs may be investing in them using bank provided leverage, in which case the risk could just boomerang right back into the banking system. Frankly, when I read about this, it triggered my PTSD from the AIG situation, with credit default swaps during the financial crisis. Now, I understand the Federal Reserve plays a role in approving SRTs, in fact, issued guidance to US Banks regarding their issuance last fall.

Could you say a little bit about the ways that these investments can be a safe way for banks to offload risk and in what ways they could become a dangerous source of contagion?


JEROME POWELL:
Sure. So there could be a breakdown in a couple of places in the chain, as you're obviously aware. One is just that the risk isn't really well and fully transferred to the buyer. And the first step is, is that risk going away off the balance sheet in an unconditional kind of a way, in a way that the bank understands.

And that's a good thing if banks are able to do that. Then the question is, is it coming back through the back door with financing. And so we're well aware of that. Banks do tend to bring these things to us, and we look at them carefully. We understand some of the ways it can go wrong. But at the end of the day, if it works to reduce the risk on a bank's balance sheet, that's something we should be okay with.


BILL FOSTER:
But there's the -- well, if you just, what sort of insight and control does the Federal Reserve have into all the connections, particularly when the connection may go through businesses that you do not have direct -- non-bank entities that you may not have direct oversight over?


JEROME POWELL:
My understanding is that this is a very active dialog we're having with banks. They want to know how this is going to be treated. They don't want to do something that is going to come back on them or that we will deny the treatment on. So I think there's a pretty transparent set of exchanges around how these things work.

We're very clear on what we think our requirements are. This is what I'm told, and so we saw what went wrong the last time. I think no one wants to repeat that, including the banks.


BILL FOSTER:
